Here is an evaluation of the candidate moves:
1.  **g7g6**: This would kick the Knight on f5, which is desirable. However, the pawn at h6 is currently defended by the pawn at g7. Moving `g7g6` leaves h6 undefended against the Knight on f5, allowing `Nxh6+` (winning a pawn and checking the King). Thus, this is a tactical error.
2.  **d7b6**: This develops the Knight and attacks the White Knight on c4. However, the Knight on d7 is the sole defender of the Bishop on e5. If the Knight moves, White can play `Nxe5` freely, winning a piece (since the Black Knight is no longer there to recapture, and the White Knight moves to e5, preventing any "counter-capture" on c4). Thus, this is a blunder.
3.  **e5f6**: Retreating the Bishop to f6 is a safe, solid option. It maintains control of the long diagonal and keeps the King safe. However, it is somewhat passive.
4.  **b7b5**: This is an aggressive pawn push. It attacks the White Knight on c4.
    *   **Safety**: The square b5 is potentially attacked by the White Bishop on f1 (diagonal f1-a6). However, the White Knight on c4 is physically blocking this diagonal, so the Bishop cannot capture on b5. No other White pieces attack b5.
    *   **Tactics**: If White responds by taking the Bishop (`Nxe5`), the White Knight moves from c4 to e5. Black can then recapture with `Nxe5`. The result is an even trade (Bishop for Knight), but Black improves their position by placing a Knight on the strong central square e5 and maintaining the space-gaining pawn on b5. If White retreats the Knight, Black has gained space and tempo.
    *   **Strategy**: It attacks the strongest white piece (Knight on c4) and initiates queenside counterplay against the White King situated on c2.

Therefore, `b7b5` is the most dynamic and advantageous move, forcing a favorable trade or displacement of the White Knight.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position is critical for Black. Black is significantly down in material (two Rooks and a Bishop for White versus a Bishop for Black), but Black has a very dangerous passed pawn on **d2**, just one square away from promotion.

The threats are:
1.  The pawn on **d2** is attacked by the White Rook on **d1** and the White King on **e2**.
2.  The pawn is currently blocked by the Rook on **d1**.
3.  If Black does not defend the pawn immediately, White will capture it (`Rxd2` or `Kxd2`), leading to a completely lost game.

We need to find a move that defends the **d2** pawn or creates a counter-threat.

Let's analyze the move **...Be1** (`g3e1`):
1.  **Defense:** The Bishop on **e1** defends the pawn on **d2**. If White plays `Rxd2`, Black can respond with `Bxd2`, capturing the Rook. This forces White to trade a Rook for the Bishop and Pawn, which is a much better scenario for Black than losing the pawn for free.
2.  **Tactical Immunity:**
    *   **King Capture:** White's King on **e2** *cannot* capture the Bishop on **e1** (`Kxe1`) because the square **e1** is defended by the Black pawn on **d2** (pawns capture diagonally forward, and Black pawns move from rank 7 to rank 1). Moving the King to **e1** would be moving into check, which is illegal.
    *   **Rook Capture:** If the White Rook on **d1** captures the Bishop (`Rxe1`), the **d1** square becomes empty. This unblocks the **d2** pawn, allowing Black to play `d2-d1=Q` (promoting to a Queen) on the next move, which would be winning for Black.

Therefore, **Be1** is the only move that keeps Black in the game by tactically defending the passed pawn.

make_move g3e1

The current position arises from the Sokolsky Opening (or Orangutan Opening) after the moves 1. b4 e5 2. Bb2 Bxb4 3. a3. Black's bishop on b4 is under attack by the pawn on a3.

I have a few options for the bishop:
1.  **`b4e7` (Be7)**: This is the most solid and principled retreat. It places the bishop on a safe square where it contributes to kingside defense and development, unblocks the d-pawn, and prepares for ...Nf6. Although it allows White to recover the pawn with Bxe5, Black achieves easy equality and comfortable development.
2.  **`b4a5` (Ba5)**: This keeps the bishop active on the queenside. However, it can sometimes be sidelined, and given the lack of a white b-pawn, the diagonal is less critical to control from a distance compared to a central post.
3.  **`b4d6` (Bd6)**: This move defends the e5 pawn, attempting to hold onto the extra material (since White lost the b-pawn). However, it blocks the d-pawn, severely hampering Black's queenside development and central control. In professional play, obstructing one's own development for a pawn in the opening is often risky and theoretically inferior.
4.  **`b4c5` (Bc5)**: An active square attacking f2, but after Bxe5, White has central presence, and the bishop is exposed.

Given standard opening principles and the specific position, **Be7** is the most reliable choice to consolidate Black's position.

The current board status shows a game in the middlegame. Black has a pawn on d4 which is in tension with White's pawn on e3. White has played an aggressive g4, but also has a pawn on h3. Black's Knight is on f6.

Analysis of candidate moves:
1.  **`d4e3`**: Black captures the white pawn on e3. This is a logical move to resolve the central tension, open the e-file, and create structural weaknesses for White (e.g., if `fxe3`, White has a backward e-pawn and an exposed king position; if `Qxe3`, the Queen is drawn into the center where it can be targeted).
2.  **`b8c6`**: Developing the Knight to a natural square, controlling the center (e5, d4). This maintains the tension. If White plays `exd4`, Black can recapture with `Nxd4` or `cxd4`.
3.  **`f6g4`**: Capturing the pawn on g4. However, White has a pawn on h3 which defends g4. `Nxg4` followed by `hxg4` loses a piece for a pawn.
4.  **`c8g4`**: Moves the Bishop to g4, but it is immediately captured by the h3 pawn.
5.  **`h7h6`**: Prophylactic move preventing further expansion or pinning, but slow.

Between `d4e3` and `b8c6`, `d4e3` is the most concrete and forcing move, capitalizing on the instability in the center and White's loose setup. It forces a response from White and potentially exposes the White King or Queen.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current board position shows a Sicilian-like structure where Black has castled short and the d-pawns have been exchanged (or Black's d-pawn is missing). White has played an aggressive and seemingly unsupported move, **g4**, pushing a pawn on the kingside flank while their King is still in the center.

Key analysis points:
1.  **Hanging Pawn on g4**: The white pawn on g4 is attacked by Black's Knight on f6 and Bishop on c8. It appears to have no defenders (the Knight on f3 does not defend g4, nor does the Queen on d2 or other pawns).
2.  **Threat of g5**: If Black does not react, White's next move could be g5, kicking the f6 Knight which is a crucial defender of the e5 pawn and the kingside castled position. Removing the g4 pawn eliminates this threat.
3.  **Candidate Moves**:
    *   `f6g4` (Nxg4): This wins the pawn but moves the Knight away from f6. The Knight on f6 is the sole defender of the e5 pawn against White's Knight on f3. If Black plays ...Nxg4, White can respond with Nxe5, restoring material balance and centralizing their knight.
    *   `c6d4` (Nd4): A strong positional move, planting the knight in the center and attacking c2 and f3. However, it leaves the g4-g5 threat unresolved.
    *   `c8g4` (Bxg4): This move wins the pawn, prevents the g5 push, and develops the bishop to an active square. Crucially, it leaves the Knight on f6 to continue protecting the e5 pawn.

**Conclusion**: Taking the pawn with the bishop (`c8g4`) is the most principled move. It punishes White's loose play, wins a pawn, stops White's kingside expansion, and maintains Black's central stability.

make_move c8g4

1.  **Analyze the Pin**: Black has a Rook on d8 and White has a Queen on d1. There is a White Pawn on d3. Since there are no other pieces between d8 and d3, or d3 and d1, the White Pawn on d3 is absolutely pinned to the Queen. It cannot move off the d-file (e.g., it cannot capture diagonally).
2.  **Evaluate Candidate Moves**:
    *   **`e5e4`**: This pawn push attacks the White Knight on f3. Normally, White might respond with `d3xe4`, but this is an illegal move because of the pin. White also cannot play `Nxe5` profitably because after `...Nxe5`, the White d-pawn still cannot capture back. This forces the White Knight to retreat, surrendering space and tempo, or results in the loss of the Knight if White tries to capture.
    *   **`f6e4`**: Moving the Knight to e4 would likely be a blunder. White could simply play `Nxe4`. Black cannot recapture with the d-pawn (as it's on d8/d7, or actually Black has no pawn on d-file impacting this immediately, but the e5 pawn is blocked). Black loses a piece.
    *   **`c5c4`**: This attacks the pinned pawn on d3, but attacking the Knight with `e5e4` is more forcing and aggressive.
    *   **`f6g4`**: This would lose the Knight to `h3xg4`.

Therefore, the strongest move is to advance the e-pawn, exploiting the pin on d3 to attack the White Knight.

make_move e5e4

White can check with the Bishop via c4 (diagonal a2-g8).

Candidate moves analysis:
1.  **d4b4**: This move puts the Rook on b4. While it would block the b-file, the White pawn on a3 protects the b4 square (a3xb4). Thus, this loses the Rook immediately.
2.  **d4c4**: Moves Rook to c4 giving check (`Rc4+`). However, White's Bishop on f1 covers c4 (via e2-d3-c4). White would simply play `Bxc4`, winning the Rook for a Bishop. This simplifies into a lost endgame.
3.  **d4e4**: Captures the e4 pawn (`Rxe4`). The e4 pawn is defended by the f3 pawn. `Rxe4` is met by `fxe4`, losing the Rook.
4.  **d4a4**: Attacks the undefended a3 pawn. This creates a threat and potentially wins a pawn. However, it ignores the threat to b7.
5.  **b7b6**: This pawn move creates a defensive chain (a7-b6-c5).
    *   It saves the b7 pawn from the threat of `Rxb7`.
    *   The b6 pawn is supported by the a7 pawn, so if White exchanges (`Rxb6 axb6`), the structure holds.
    *   It also provides support to the c5 pawn.
    *   Keeping the position closed is generally favorable when defending against superior firepower (two Rooks).

Given the material deficit, Black needs to play solidly and avoid immediate tactical blunders. `b7b6` shores up the queenside pawn structure and negates the immediate threat on the b-file.

Analyzing candidate moves:

2.  **d4c4+**: Moving to c4 checks the King. However, the square c4 is attacked by the White Bishop on f1 (light-squared bishop controls the f1-a6 diagonal, which includes c4). Playing ...Rc4+ allows Bxc4, leading to further material loss.
3.  **d4d1**: Attacks the Bishop on f1, but square d1 is protected by the King on c2. Lose Rook.
4.  **d4e4**: Moves to e4. The square e4 is attacked by the White Pawn on f3. Lose Rook.
5.  **d4g4**: Moves to g4. The square g4 is attacked by both White Pawns f3 and h3. Lose Rook.
6.  **d4f4**: The Rook moves to f4. This square is safe:
    - Not attacked by Pawn f3 (attacks e4, g4).
    - Not attacked by Pawn f5 (attacks e6, g6).
    - Not attacked by Bishop f1 (light-squared, f4 is dark).
    - Not attacked by King or Rooks.
    From f4, the Black Rook attacks the **undefended White Pawn on f3** and the **undefended White Pawn on f5**. This creates a double threat (forking the pawns). White will struggle to defend both.
    - If White defends f3 (e.g., Rf2), Black can capture f5 (...Rxf5).
    - If White defends f5 (hard to do), Black can capture f3 (...Rxf3).
    Winning a pawn and exposing the White King further is the best strategic option.

Other moves like pawn pushes (c5c4, g5g4) or passive moves don't offer the same immediate tactical gain or activity.

The move `f5c5` transfers the Rook to the c-file. This is a strong positional move for several reasons:
1.  **X-Ray Attack/Battery**: It places the Rook on the same file as the White King (c2). Although the Black pawn on c4 currently blocks the line of sight, this sets up a discovered check. If the pawn moves (e.g., `c4-c3+`), the Rook will deliver a check to the King.
2.  **Support**: The Rook on c5 supports the advance of the c-pawn and controls important squares on the 5th rank.
3.  **Safety**: The square c5 is not attacked by any White pieces (White pawns are on a3 and h3).
4.  **Coordination**: It coordinates well with the Bishop on f6, which protects the potential `c3` square.

Other moves like `f5f2+` are bad because the White Rook on h2 simply captures the Rook (`Rxf2`). Pawn moves like `c4c3` immediately are decent but `Rc5` prepares a more potent threat and improves the worst-placed major piece.

    *   **Black:** King on g8, Bishop on f6, Rook on c5, Pawns on c4, b5, g5, etc.

    *   **Protection:** The square c3 is on the long diagonal of the Black Bishop at f6 (f6-e5-d4-c3). This means the pawn on c3 is defended by the Bishop.
    *   **Safety:** The White King is on c2, adjacent to c3. Normally, the King might capture the pawn. **Alternative `f6c3`:**

1.  **Exchanges material**: Trading the Knight for the Bishop is an even trade materially, but it removes a defender of White's shaky position.
2.  **Opens lines**: Moving the Knight from d4 unblocks the d-file, allowing the Rook on d8 to exert pressure on the d3 pawn and the King on d2 behind it. It also clears the diagonal for the g7 Bishop (once the e5 pawn moves).

The move **f5f4** is the most promising candidate for the following reasons:
1.  **Forces Line Opening**: The pawn on f4 attacks both e3 and g3. White cannot easily ignore this because `fxe3+` (capturing the pawn on e3) would deliver a check to the King on d2 and rip open the position.
2.  **Favorable Exchanges**: White is virtually forced to capture on f4 (e.g., `exf4`, `gxf4`). After `exf4` by White and `exf4` by Black (recapturing with the e5 pawn), Black maintains the material balance (trading two pawns for two) while opening the f-file for the Rook and potentially the diagonal for the Bishop on g7.
3.  **Tactical Safety**: Black's pawn on f4 (after the exchange sequence `1... f4 2. exf4 exf4`) is defended by the Rook on f8. If White attempts to win a pawn with `3. Qxf4`, Black can respond with `3... Rxf4 4. gxf4`, winning the Queen for a Rook and a Pawn, which is a decisive material advantage for Black.

Other moves like `e5e4` tend to lose a pawn (after `dxe4 fxe4 Qxe4`) without as much compensation. Developing moves like `g6g5` are playable but less forcing.

However, this move does not attack any of Black's pieces with tempo (the g7 Bishop is safe). Black keeps the g7 Bishop in place to defend the King. Although the d5 Bishop is momentarily out of play, Black has gained a full Rook for a Bishop (or potentially just wins the Exchange if the Bishop is eventually trapped and exchanged), which is a winning material advantage.

Therefore, `d5h1` is the superior capture as it avoids giving White a tempo-gaining developing move and maintains better King safety.

    *   While good positionally, it is not as forcing as `d7d4` and does not win material immediately.

3.  **Safety**: After `Qxa1`, the Black Queen is in the corner but not immediately trapped. White's pieces are uncoordinated, and the White Queen on h2 is blocked by the f4 pawn from attacking the Black King or aiding the defense effectively.
4.  **Alternative Moves**:
    *   `d4g1` (Qxg1+): This is a check, but the square g1 is defended by the White Queen on h2. This would lead to the loss of the Black Queen.
    *   `e8e1` (Re1+): This is also a check, but the square e1 is defended by the White King. This leads to the loss of a Rook.
    *   `h1f3`: The Bishop moving to f3 attacks the King, but f3 is defended by the White Knight on g1.
    *   Capturing pawns (e.g., `d4f4`, `d4a4`, `d4d3`) leads to the loss of the Queen or is less valuable than the Rook.

Therefore, capturing the undefended rook on a1 is the best move.

make_move d4a1

The most prudent strategy is to simplify the position by trading Queens, which eliminates any potential counterplay from White and leads to an easily winning endgame.

The move `b3d5` places the Black Queen on d5, supported by the Rook on d8. This move:
1.  Attacks the White Queen on e4.
2.  Offers a Queen trade. If White plays `Qxd5`, Black responds with `Rxd5`.
3.  Centralizes the pieces and leverages the open d-file.
4.  Is safe because the d5 square is protected by the Rook, and the path from b3 to d5 is clear.

If White declines the trade, they must move their Queen, likely giving up further ground or material. Given the material advantage, forcing a simplification is the best move.

make_move b3d5

Black's goal is to convert the material advantage by restricting White's King, creating threats, or simplifying into a winning endgame.

Candidate moves evaluation:
1.  **`d5g2`**: The Queen invades White's position.
    *   **Attacks**: It attacks the Knight on g1. The Knight is currently defended by the White Queen on e3.
    *   **Safety**: The square g2 is not attacked by the White King (e2), Queen (e3), or Knight (g1). It is a safe outpost.

4.  **`c5c4`**: Pushes a pawn to attack the base of White's structure (d3). This is a reasonable positional move but `d5g2` is more forcing and active.

Given the analysis, **`d5g2`** is the strongest move. It is safe, active, restricts the opponent, and attacks a piece, potentially forcing a favorable simplification.

1.  **Attacks the Queen**: The Rook moves to b3, directly attacking the White Queen on b7.
2.  **Protected**: The Rook on b3 is protected by the pawn at c4. If the White Queen captures the Rook (`Qxb3`), Black recaptures with the pawn (`cxb3`). The alternative `e3d3+` (Rd3+) is also a protected check, but forcing the King to move is less decisive than attacking the Queen or forcing a winning trade. The move `e3b3` puts the question to the Queen immediately.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position shows the Black Rook at d3, which is under potential threat from the White King at e2 (though momentarily protected by the Black Pawn at c4, as Black pawns capture "down" towards rank 1). However, Black intends to push the c-pawn (`c4c3`), which would remove this protection and leave the Rook vulnerable or blocking the pawn. Furthermore, the square d4 is unsafe because the White Queen at a7 controls the a7-g1 diagonal (a7-b6-c5-d4).

The move `d3d5` places the Rook on a safe, active square. From d5, the Rook:
1.  Maintains the "cutoff" along the d-file, preventing the White King from easily crossing to the queenside to stop the c-pawn.
2.  Avoids the dangerous a7-g1 diagonal.
3.  Clears the path for the c-pawn to advance.
4.  Prepares for potential checks or lateral movement along the 5th rank.

Other rook moves like `d3d2+` or `d3e3` are blunders as they allow the King to capture the Rook (d2 is not protected by c4, and moving to d2 removes the rook from d3). `d3d4` is a blunder due to `Qxd4`. `d3d5` is the most logical and strong move.

make_move d3d5

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position shows the Black Knight on c1 is under attack by both the White King on d1 and the White Queen on c8 (via the c-file). If the Knight does not move, it will be captured, leaving Black in a lost position (Queen vs Pawns).

Black must move the Knight to a safe square.
- `c1e2` puts the Knight on e2, where it is still attacked by the White King on d1.
- `c1a2` moves the Knight to the edge of the board, which is passive.
- `c1b3` and `c1d3` are both safe squares that save the Knight.

Comparing `c1b3` and `c1d3`:
- `c1d3` centralizes the Knight, placing it closer to the Black King and the Kingside pawns, where it can participate in defense or create counterplay. It also restricts the White King's movement to c1 and e1.
- `c1b3` moves towards the Queenside.

`c1d3` is generally the more active and centralizing move.

make_move c1d3

f6g5,f6e5,d3e5,d3c5,d3f4,d3b4,d3f2,d3b2,d3e1,d3c1,h6h5,g6g5

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position features a White Queen on d7, King on c2, and Pawn on a4 against a Black King on f6, Knight on d3, and Pawns on g6, h6.

Black's Knight on d3 is well-placed to jump to e5 (`d3e5`), where it will:
1.  **Attack the White Queen** on d7, forcing her to move.
2.  **Centralize the Knight**, controlling key squares like c4, c6, d7, f7, g6, g4, f3.
3.  **Improve coordination** with the Black King. If White responds with a check like `Qd6+`, the Black King can step to f5 (`Kf5`), where it protects the Knight on e5.

Alternative move `d3c5` also forks the Queen and the a4 pawn. However, after `1... Nc5 2. Qd6+`, the Black King is forced to move, and the Knight on c5 would be undefended (or pinned if `2... Ne6` is played), leading to a passive position or material loss.

The check `d3b4+` forces the White King to move but drives the Knight to the edge of the board without creating lasting threats.

Therefore, `d3e5` is the strongest move, gaining a tempo by attacking the Queen and placing the Knight on a supported, central square.

make_move d3e5

White attacks the Knight with the Queen. The Knight is defended by the Black King at f6. To improve the position and save the Knight from being "paralyzed" or lost if more attackers arrive, Black should move the King to a square where it still defends the Knight but removes the pin.

The available King moves that maintain defense of the Knight (at e5) are:
1.  **f6e6**: The King moves to e6. This square is adjacent to the Knight at e5, so the King continues to protect it. Moving the King off the diagonal unpins the Knight. From e6, the King is fairly central and restricts White's Queen checks (e.g., covering d6 and f5).
2.  **f6f5**: The King moves to f5. This also defends the Knight and unpins. However, f5 is more exposed to checks from the White Queen (e.g., Qd3+ or Qf2+), which could force the King away from the Knight or into a worse position.

Comparing the two, **f6e6** is safer because it places the King on a square less vulnerable to immediate checks while unpinning and maintaining protection of the Knight. Waiting with a pawn move (like h6h5) leaves the pin in place and allows White to improve their position or give checks (like Qd6+).

Therefore, the best move is to step out of the pin while keeping the Knight defended.

make_move f6e6

The move **c6d4** (Knight to d4) appears to be the strongest for the following reasons:
1.  **Blocking the Queen**: Placing the Knight on d4 blocks the long diagonal a1-h8. This cuts off the White Queen from defending the queenside (specifically squares like b2 and c3) or checking the King on the diagonal.
2.  **Safety**: The Knight on d4 is protected by the Black King on d5. If White plays `Qxd4+`, `Kxd4` leaves Black with a winning King and Pawn endgame vs King and Pawn (Black has two pawns vs one).
3.  **Threats**: From d4, the Knight attacks c2 and b3. It threatens `Nc2+`, checking the King on a1. Because the diagonal is blocked, the White Queen cannot return to b2 to defend against this threat (unlike if Black played `c6b4`, where `Qb2` would be possible).
4.  **Centralization**: The Knight improves its position significantly, moving closer to the White King and controlling key squares.

Other moves like `c6b4` allow White to defend with `Qb2`. `d5c4` allows White to improve their defensive setup. Therefore, `c6d4` is the most forcing and tactically sound move.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current board position shows the Black King on b3, Black Knight on d4, White King on b1, and White Queen on h6. White also has a pawn on a4.
Black is under threat from the White Queen, which has a potential checking square on e3 (h6-e3 diagonal).
Black needs to find a move that improves their position, potentially by creating threats or preventing White's attack.

Let's analyze the candidate moves:
1.  **...Nf5 (d4f5)**:
    *   The Knight moves to f5.
    *   From f5, the Knight attacks the White Queen on h6.
    *   From f5, the Knight also covers the e3 square (5,4 -> 4,2 is a knight's move). This prevents the White Queen from delivering a check on e3.
    *   This move gains a tempo by attacking the Queen and simultaneously defends against a check.

2.  **...Nc2 (d4c2)**:
    *   The Knight moves to c2.
    *   From c2, the Knight attacks a1 and e3.
    *   It prevents the check on e3 (since 2. Qe3+ Nxe3).
    *   It attacks a1, which, combined with the Black King's control of a2/b2, forces the White King to move to c1 (2. Kc1).
    *   While forcing the King to move is good, the White King moving to c1 might not be disadvantageous for White, and the Queen remains free to reposition.

3.  **Other moves**:
    *   ...Ne2 (d4e2) attacks c1 but allows 2. Qe3+.
    *   ...Kxa4 (b3a4) captures a pawn but leaves the King exposed and loses tempo.

Comparing ...Nf5 and ...Nc2:
*   ...Nf5 creates a direct threat against the most valuable piece (the Queen). This forces White to move the Queen, giving Black the initiative.
*   ...Nc2 forces a King move but doesn't threaten a high-value piece directly.
*   Both prevent the dangerous Qe3+ check.

Given that ...Nf5 attacks the Queen and defends against the check, it is the most active and strong defensive resource.

b3c4,b3b4,b3a4,b3c3,b3a3,b3c2,a2b4,a2c3,a2c1,h5h4

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current board state features the White King on a1, White Queen on g5, and White Pawn on a5. Black has the King on b3, Knight on a2, and Pawns on g6 and h5.

Black's goal is to checkmate the White King or achieve a winning material advantage. The White King is currently restricted in the corner.
- The square a2 is occupied by the Black Knight.
- The square b2 is controlled by the Black King on b3.
- The square b1 is currently the only escape square for the White King (as the Black King on b3 does not control b1).

Let's analyze the move **Nc3** (`a2c3`):
1.  The Knight moves from a2 to c3.
2.  From c3, the Knight controls the squares a2, b1, d1, e2, a4, b5, d5, and e4.
3.  Crucially, the Knight now controls **b1**.
4.  The Black King on b3 continues to control **a2** and **b2**.
5.  With the Knight on c3 and King on b3, the White King on a1 has **no legal moves**:
    -   a2 is empty but controlled by the Black King.
    -   b2 is controlled by the Black King.
    -   b1 is controlled by the Black Knight.
6.  This creates a "mating net" or prison for the King. White is not in stalemate because White has other legal moves (Queen and Pawn).
7.  Additionally, the Knight on c3 attacks important squares like d1 and e2, restricting the White Queen's movement.

Comparing this to other moves:
-   `b3c2` (Kc2) also traps the King (covers b1, b2; a2 is occupied by Knight), but it leaves the Knight passive on a2.
-   `a2c1` (Nc1) blocks c1 but leaves b1 uncontrolled and a2 empty (though a2 is attacked by Nc1, it's not sufficient if the King moves there? Actually Nc1 attacks a2, but allows Kb1).
-   `a2b4` (Nb4) allows the King to escape to b1 or a2.

Therefore, `a2c3` is the strongest move as it completely immobilizes the White King and activates the Knight for future threats or capturing the Queen if she moves to a forked square.

make_move a2c3

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position shows the Black King on b3, Black Knight on a2, and Black Pawn on h5. The White King is trapped in the corner at a1, with a White Queen on g5 and Pawn on a5.

The Black King on b3 controls the escape squares a2 and b2. The square b1 is the only escape for the White King, unless it is covered.
The move `b3c2` moves the Black King to c2. From c2, the King covers b1, b2, and c1, while maintaining the blockade of the White King (since a2 is occupied by the Black Knight).
This move leaves the White King with no legal moves (a1 is the only square, and it has no liberties).
White must therefore move the Queen or the a-pawn.
The move `b3c2` also places the King in a position that protects c1 and d2, discouraging White Queen checks (e.g., `Qc1+` or `Qd2+` would be met by `KxQ`, and even though it might lead to a pawn race, Black has the advantage or can avoid the capture if preferred).
Other moves like `a2c3` (Knight to c3) also trap the king but allow the White Queen to deliver a check on d2, forcing the Black King to move and likely releasing the trap on the White King.
Moves like `a2c1` or `b3a3` allow the White King to escape to b1.

Therefore, `b3c2` is the most restrictive and strongest move, maintaining the mating net and allowing Black to advance the h-pawn or force a decisive advantage.

make_move b3c2

Proxy (to Player_Black):

g4h5,g4g5,g4f5,g4h4,g4f4,g4g3,h3h2

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position shows a White Queen on h1, White King on b1, and a White Pawn on a5. Black has a King on g4 and a Pawn on h3.

Black's pawn on h3 is currently preventing the White Queen from having free reign of the kingside, but it is also vulnerable.
- If Black plays `h3h2`, the pawn moves to h2 where it is attacked by the White Queen on h1. The Black King on g4 does not protect h2, so White can simply play `Qxh2+`, winning the pawn and checking the King, leading to an easy win.
- If Black moves the King away (e.g., `g4f4`, `g4f5`, etc.), the pawn on h3 is left undefended and can be captured by the Queen (`Qxh3`).
- If Black plays `g4g3`, the King moves to support the pawn. From g3, the King protects h3. If White were to capture the pawn (`Qxh3+`), the Black King could recapture (`Kxh3`), forcing a King + Pawn ending (which White still wins due to the a-pawn, but it forces White to give up the Queen). Therefore, White cannot take the pawn immediately and must maneuver to win. This prolongs the game and is the most resilient defensive move.

Thus, `g4g3` is the best move available.

make_move g4g3

Proxy (to Player_Black):

g3h4,g3g4,g3f4,g3f2,h3h2

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position is a lost endgame for Black, as White has a Queen and a passed 'a' pawn, while Black only has a King and a blocked 'h' pawn. Black's only hope is to prolong the game and hope for a blunder (like a stalemate).

Current Status:
- White: King at c2, Queen at h1, Pawn at a5.
- Black: King at g3, Pawn at h3.

Analysis of Candidate Moves:
1.  **h3h2**: The pawn advances to h2. White would simply respond with `Qxh2+`, forcing `Kxh2`. The resulting King vs. King + Pawn ending is a trivial win for White (the Black King is too far from the 'a' pawn). If White doesn't take, the pawn is blocked by the Queen and cannot promote, leaving it useless.
2.  **g3f2**: This moves the King towards the center/White's camp but abandons the protection of the h3 pawn. White would play `Qxh3`, eliminating Black's last piece and leading to a simple checkmate.
3.  **g3f4**: Similarly, this abandons the h3 pawn, leading to `Qxh3` and a quick loss.
4.  **g3h4**: Moves the King to the edge of the board. While it protects the pawn, it limits the King's mobility.
5.  **g3g4**: Moves the King to a slightly more central square while maintaining protection of the h3 pawn. This keeps the "threat" of the pawn alive (forcing the White Queen to monitor it) and maximizes the King's activity, which is the standard principle when resisting in a lost position.

Therefore, `g3g4` is the most resilient move.

make_move g3g4

Proxy (to Player_Black):

g3h4,g3g4,g3f4,g3h2,g3f2,h3h2

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position is a classic endgame scenario: King and Pawn vs King and Queen.
White has a Queen at a8 and a King at c2.
Black has a King at g3 and a Pawn at h3.
Black's pawn is moving towards h1 to promote.

In King + Pawn vs King + Queen endgames, the side with the pawn can often achieve a draw if:
1.  The pawn is a rook pawn (a or h file) or a bishop pawn (c or f file).
2.  The pawn has reached the 7th rank (relative to the moving side, so 2nd rank on the board for Black).
3.  The defending King is close to the promotion square.
4.  The attacking King is far away.

Here, Black's pawn is a rook pawn (h-file). It is currently at h3. Moving it to h2 places it one square away from promotion (equivalent to the 7th rank for Black). The Black King is at g3, supporting the pawn. The White King is at c2, which is far away from the action.

If Black plays **h3h2**, the pawn threatens to promote. The White Queen cannot simply capture the pawn (protected by the King) or easily checkmate. Because it is a rook pawn, White often cannot force the Black King out of the corner (h1) without causing a stalemate (since h1 is a corner, the King has limited squares, and blocking the pawn often leaves no legal moves).

Therefore, pushing the pawn to h2 offers the best chance for a draw. Any other move (moving the King) allows White to bring their King closer or improve the Queen's position without the immediate threat of promotion or the stalemate defense.

make_move h3h2

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position shows a black pawn on h2 and the black king on g3. White has a queen on b7, a king on c2, and a pawn on a6. The black pawn is one square away from promotion on h1.

Black's legal moves include moving the king (g3h4, g3g4, g3f4, g3h3, g3f2) or promoting the pawn (h2h1q, h2h1r, h2h1b, h2h1n).

Analyzing the position:
The white queen on b7 controls the long diagonal (b7-c6-d5-e4-f3-g2-h1). This means the promotion square h1 is under direct observation by the white queen. Additionally, the squares f3 and g2 on this diagonal are controlled by the queen, preventing the black king (currently on g3) from stepping onto them to block the line of sight.

If Black promotes with `h2h1q` (or any other piece), White will respond with `Qxh1`, capturing the newly promoted piece. Black will be left with only a king against White's king, queen, and pawn, resulting in a lost endgame.
If Black plays a king move like `g3f2`, the pawn remains on h2. White can simply maneuver their king or queen to win the pawn or deliver checkmate.

In such forced loss situations, promoting to a queen is the most principled move, as it forces the opponent to execute the capture and changes the material balance temporarily, even if the outcome is inevitable.

make_move h2h1q

The current position shows Black with a strong development advantage and control of the center (pawns on d5, e5; knights on c6, f6; bishop on c5), while White is largely undeveloped but has pushed a pawn to h6. This h6 pawn is an aggressive thorn aimed at the black kingside, but it also currently blocks the h-file for the White rook on h1.

Analysis of candidate moves:
1.  **`g7h6` (gxh6)**: This would capture the annoying pawn but opens the h-file. Since White has a rook on h1, this would allow `Rxh6`, activating White's rook and creating immediate threats against Black's kingside. It is safer to keep the h-file closed. The white pawn on h6 actually shields the black king from the white rook.
2.  **`d5d4`**: This pushes in the center, attacking e3. White would likely respond with `exd4`, leading to exchanges. While good, securing the king first is usually preferred when facing a flank attack.
3.  **`c8g4`**: Develops the bishop to an active square. A solid move, but castling is more critical to ensure safety.
4.  **`e8g8` (O-O)**: Castling kingside moves the King to g8 and the Rook to f8. With the King on g8, the pawns on f7, g7, and h7 (and the blocking white pawn on h6) provide good shelter. As established, the h6 pawn blocks the h-file, so the king is safe from immediate rook attacks. This move consolidates Black's advantage by securing the king, allowing Black to focus on exploiting the center and development lead.

Given the position, castling is the most professional and principled response to White's premature flank aggression.

- This forces White to make concessions, such as moving the Queen to unpin (e.g., Qe2), leading to exchanges that favor Black's active pieces and central control, or potentially losing material if they cannot break the pin in time.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position shows black having a significant advantage with a Queen against no Queen for White (assuming standard piece values and displayed pieces). Black has a strong attack brewing against the White King on e1. The Black Bishop on e3 and Knight on g4 are coordinating against the f2 square, which is only defended by the King and the Rook on g2 (sideways defense).

The move `e3f2` (Bxf2+) is a decisive tactical strike.
1. It is a check, forcing White to respond immediately.
2. It captures a pawn, further opening lines to the White King.
3. If White captures with the Rook (`Rxf2`), Black responds with `Nxf2`, winning the exchange (Rook for Knight/Bishop) and keeping the attack alive.
4. If White captures with the King (`Kxf2`), the King is drawn into the center, exposed to further checks from the Black Queen (e.g., `Qf6+` or `Qh4+`) and Knight.

Given the material advantage and the exposed White King, forcing the game with a capture-check is the optimal strategy.

- Black has a Knight on **e3** and a Bishop on **f2**.
- White has a King on **e2**, Rooks on **a2** and **g2**, and a Bishop on **c3**.
- The Black Knight on e3 is attacking several squares, including the White Rook on **g2**.
- The Black Bishop on f2 is currently under attack by the White King and the White Rook on g2.

Evaluating candidate moves:
1.  **e3g2** (Nxg2): This captures the White Rook. This wins significant material (Rook for Knight/Bishop exchange). After 1... Nxg2, White will likely respond with 2. Kxf2 (King takes Bishop). Black trades two pieces (N+B, approx 6 pts) for a Rook (5 pts)? Wait, after 2. Kxf2, the Knight on g2 is also under attack by the King. However, having a Rook advantage when White has no Queen (not visible on board) is very strong.
    Actually, let's re-evaluate the trade.
    Current state: B(f2) is en prise (attacked by K & R). N(e3) is en prise (attacked by K).
    If Black does nothing, Black loses material.
    If 1... Nxg2, Black gains 5 points immediately.
    If White recaptures 2. Kxf2, Black has lost the Bishop but eliminated a Rook. The Knight on g2 might be trapped or lost, but taking the Rook is the most forcing and rewarding move available. Given White lacks a Queen, simplifying by removing a Rook is advantageous.
    Also, if Black tries to save the Bishop, the Knight on e3 is lost.
    Therefore, capturing the Rook is the best way to convert the position.

2.  **Qd8 moves**:
    - **d8h4**: This would be a mistake as the White Pawn on g3 covers h4.
    - **d8d3** (Qxd3+): The pawn on d3 is protected by the King and Pawn at c2. Losing the Queen is bad.

The move **e3g2** captures a heavy piece and is the most tactically sound decision.

Therefore, I will play the Knight to d4.

The available moves for the knight on g2 are `g2h4`, `g2f4`, `g2e3`, and `g2e1`.
- `g2h4` and `g2f4` move the knight to squares covered by the white pawn on g3, leading to the loss of the knight (gxh4 or gxf4).
- `g2e1` moves the knight to the back rank where `Kxe1` is possible.
- `g2e3` moves the knight to a central square. If the king does not capture, the knight remains on a strong square, likely to be supported by `d4f5` on the next turn.

Therefore, `g2e3` is the most resourceful move, creating tactical complications and either saving the piece or selling it for a strong attack on the exposed king.
